Note
Symbols of United Nations documents are composed of letters combined with
figures. Mention of such a symbol indicates a reference to a United Nations document.
The resolutions and decisions of the Security Council are published in yearly
volumes of Resolutions and Decisions of the Security Council.

A/72/2

Introduction
1.
Mindful of its primary responsibility for the
maintenance of international peace and security, the
Security Council continued to engage on a widereaching agenda in accordance with the principles and
objectives set forth in the Charter of the United Nations.
During the period under review, the Council held 296
formal meetings, of which 282 were public. The Council
adopted 61 resolutions and 27 presidential statements
and issued 93 statements to the press. During the
reporting period, the Council conducted five missions.

assessments.shtml. Records of all open Council
meetings, including the names and titles of officials
providing briefings, are available at www.un.org/en/sc/
meetings/. This introduction does not seek to provide an
exhaustive list of meetings, developments or Council
responses. It is intended as a succinct and useful
overview of key developments. Unless otherwise
indicated, resolutions referred to in this introduction
were adopted unanimously.

2.
The present report incorporates the changes
mandated in the note by the President dated 30 August
(S/2017/507), whereby all future reports will cover the
calendar year, 1 January to 31 December. This report
covers the year 2017.

Africa

3.
During the reporting period, the situation in the
Middle East figured prominently on the Council’s
agenda. Meetings were held on the situations in the
Syrian Arab Republic, Iraq and Yemen, the Palestinian
question, the United Nations Disengagement Observer
Force and Lebanon. The Council also regularly
monitored the situation in Afghanistan.
4.
The situations in Africa remained a focus of the
Council. Frequent meetings were held on the Central
African Republic, the Democratic Republic of the
Congo, Guinea-Bissau, Libya, Mali, Somalia and South
Sudan.
5.
Thematic, general and cross-cutting issues
remained a priority for the Council, including
non-proliferation, threats to international peace and
security caused by terrorist acts, the protection of
civilians in armed conflict, children and armed conflict,
women and peace and security, peacekeeping and
peacebuilding, sanctions, cooperation between the
United Nations and regional and subregional
organizations, peace and security in Africa, and the rule
of law.
6.
The Council considered the work of the
International Tribunal for the Former Yugoslavia and the
International Residual Mechanism for Criminal
Tribunals and continued to monitor situations that it had
referred to the International Criminal Court concerning
the situations in Darfur and Libya.
7.
Further information on the work of the Council
and more detailed accounts of its meetings can be found
in the monthly assessments of the Council’s work,
available online at www.un.org/en/sc/programme/

8/234

Visits of the Security Council to Africa
8.
The Council undertook three missions to Africa in
the reporting period.
9.
From 1 to 7 March, the Council sent a mission to
the countries of the Lake Chad basin region, namely
Cameroon, Chad, the Niger and Nigeria. Later in March,
the mission’s Co-Chairs briefed the Council on the
situation in the region and emphasized the need for an
enhanced and holistic response to the challenges facing
the subregion. The Council also heard from the Deputy
Secretary-General, Amina J. Mohammed, who addressed
the 15-member organ for the first time in that capacity.
10. During the second mission, from 6 to 8 September,
the Council visited Ethiopia for the 11th annual joint
consultative meeting between the members of the
United Nations Security Council and the Peace and
Security Council of the African Union at the
Headquarters of the African Union, in Addis Ababa. At
the meeting there was discussion on the situations in
Somalia, South Sudan and the Lake Chad basin. This
mission was the first stand-alone visit by the members
of the Security Council to the African Union
Headquarters in Addis Ababa, and strengthened the
partnership and enhanced cooperation between the two
Councils.
11. The third mission was to the Sahel region and
comprised visits to Mali, Mauritania and Burkina Faso,
from 19 to 22 October, to assess the operationalization
of the Joint Force of the Group of Five for the Sahel.
During the visit, Council members met with
representatives of the Governments of Mali, Mauritania
and Burkina Faso, the United Nations Multidimensional
Integrated Stabilization Mission in Mali, international
security presences in Mali (including the Joint Force,
European Union missions and French forces), civil

18-14247

-----------------------Page 10 End-----------------------

Introduction

society as well as actors involved in the implementation
of the Agreement on Peace and Reconciliation in Mali.
The Council saw evidence of great determination among
regional actors in the Sahel to overcome security
challenges through deployment of the regional force.
Burundi
12. The Council was briefed concerning the political,
socioeconomic and humanitarian situation in Burundi.
The Council noted that the Arusha Agreement must
remain the cornerstone of all efforts, and the first
priority was to promote an inclusive dialogue.
13. In August, the Council issued a presidential
statement (S/PRST/2017/13), in which it expressed
concern over the political and humanitarian situation,
noted that the security situation in Burundi had
remained generally calm, commended regional efforts to
help Burundians to find political solutions and reiterated
its support to the facilitation led by the East African
Community through the Special Envoy.
Central African region
14. The activities of Boko Haram continued to have
devastating
consequences
for
the
political,
socioeconomic, humanitarian and human rights
situations in the affected areas of Central Africa. While
progress continued in the fight against the Lord’s
Resistance Army, the group continued to demonstrate its
intention and capacity to conduct attacks against
civilians in the Central African region.
15. The Council received briefings on the Central
Africa region in June and December. The Special
Representative of the Secretary-General and Head of the
United Nations Regional Office for Central Africa
(UNOCA), briefed the Council on challenges in the
political, security, electoral and humanitarian situations
and in upholding human rights in the region and on the
implementation of the United Nations regional strategy
to combat LRA. The Council urged UNOCA to continue
to facilitate coordination among regional actors in
addressing the challenges the region faced.
Central African Republic
16. Despite the efforts made by the Government of the
Central African Republic and positive progress in the
political process coordinated by the African Union, the
security situation in the Central African Republic during
2017 continued to be critical, with an upsurge of
violence committed by the armed groups.
17. The Council issued statements to the press
expressing condemnations of attacks on the United
Nations Multidimensional Integrated Stabilization
18-14247

Mission in the Central African Republic (MINUSCA) in
January, April, May, July and November. In February,
Council members released a press statement, deploring
attacks against civilians and incitement to ethnic and
religious hatred and violence. They also reaffirmed their
support for the African Initiative for Peace and
Reconciliation in the Central African Republic as the
main framework for a political solution.
18. In February, the Under-Secretary-General for
Peacekeeping Operations briefed the Council on the
latest report of the Secretary-General on the situation in
the Central African Republic. The Chair of the Central
African Republic configuration of the Peacebuilding
Commission and the Chair of the Committee established
pursuant to resolution 2127 (2013) concerning the
Central African Republic also made statements.
19. In March, the Under-Secretary-General for
Peacekeeping Operations and the President of the
Central African Republic, Faustin Archange Touadera,
briefed the Council on the situation in the country. The
briefers stressed that international support and
partnerships were vital to the stability of the Central
African Republic.
20. In April, the Council issued a presidential
statement (S/PRST/2017/5), in which it reaffirmed the
Council’s support for President Touadera, expressed
concern at the ongoing violence between armed groups,
acknowledged reconciliation efforts, and condemned
attacks against civilians.
21. In June, the Council received a briefing from the
Special Representative of the Secretary-General for the
Central African Republic, on the implementation of the
mandate of MINUSCA. The Special Representative
described the recent increase in violence in the Central
African Republic, and insisted that MINUSCA would
maintain a robust posture towards armed groups, and
that this would need to be accompanied by increased
peacebuilding efforts.
22. In July, the Council issued a presidential statement
(S/PRST/2017/9), in which it condemned the ongoing
clashes between armed groups and reiterated its support
for President Touadera and the Government and people
of the Central African Republic.
23. The Council had another briefing on the Central
African Republic in November, which was followed by
consultations in which the Under-Secretary-General for
Peacekeeping Operations participated.
24. The Council adopted resolution 2339 (2017) in
January, by which it renewed the sanctions regime
against the Central African Republic until 31 January
2018 and the mandate of the Panel of Experts until
9/234

-----------------------Page 11 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

28 February 2018. By resolution 2387 (2017), adopted
in November, the Council renewed the mandate of
MINUSCA until 15 November 2018 and authorized a
troop increase of 900.
Côte d’Ivoire
25. In February, the Council was briefed by the
Special Representative of the Secretary-General and
Head of the United Nations Operation in Côte d’Ivoire
(UNOCI) on the latest developments in the country and
the Secretary-General’s report.
26. In June, the Special Representative of the
Secretary-General and Head of UNOCI delivered her
final briefing to the Council ahead of the mission’s
closure on 30 June. Members of the Council
commended the Government, and some stressed that
lessons learned in Côte d’Ivoire could be used for future
United Nations peacekeeping operations. Later in June,
the Council adopted a presidential statement
(S/PRST/2017/8) welcoming the closure of UNOCI.
Democratic Republic of the Congo
27. The Council held briefings and consultations
frequently on the situation in the Democratic Republic
of the Congo. In the briefings, the Council was informed
that, despite the efforts made by international partners
and the Transitional Government, political tensions in
the Democratic Republic of the Congo continued to rise,
amid continuing challenges to the full implementation
of the political agreement of 31 December 2016 and a
rising number of displaced people and an increase in
human rights abuses, which were prolonging political
uncertainty.
28. The security situation remained fragile in several
areas in the country, notably in the eastern Democratic
Republic of the Congo and the Kasaï region. The United
Nations Organization Stabilization Mission in the
Democratic Republic of the Congo (MONUSCO)
continued to readjust itself to best project force and
enhance the protection of civilians. The Council issued
a statement to the press in February, strongly
condemning violence in the Kasaï region, and in March,
in another press statement, the Council condemned the
killing of two members of the Group of Experts who
were monitoring the sanctions regime in the Kasai
Central region.
29. The Council issued three presidential statements
during 2017. In the first (S/PRST/2017/1), in January,
the Council welcomed the “comprehensive and
inclusive political agreement” signed in Kinshasa on
31 December 2016; in the second (S/PRST/2017/12), in
July, the Council stressed the urgent need for swift
10/234

implementation of that political agreement, and
condemned the violence in the Kasai region; and in the
third (S/PRST/2017/23), issued in November regarding
the investigation into the death of the two experts, the
Council reiterated the need for the Government to fully
investigate the killing of the two experts and bring the
perpetrators to justice.
30. In March, by resolution 2348 (2017), the Council
extended the mandate of MONUSCO until 31 March
2018, reducing its troop ceiling by 3,600 military
personnel to 16,215 troops, from 19,815 under its
previous mandate. The Council requested that the
Secretary-General conduct a strategic review of
MONUSCO and examine the continued relevance of its
mandated tasks, with a view to providing advice on a
further troop-level reduction and on an exit strategy.
31. In June, by resolution 2360 (2017), the Council
renewed until 1 July 2018 the measures relating to the
arms embargo, asset freeze and travel ban affecting the
Democratic Republic of the Congo, expanding those
sanctions to cover individuals and entities engaging i n
or providing support for acts that included planning,
directing, sponsoring or participating in attacks against
MONUSCO peacekeepers or United Nations personnel.
It also extended until 1 August 2018 the mandate of the
Group of Experts assisting the Committee established
pursuant to resolution 1533 (2004) concerning the
Democratic Republic of the Congo. In August, the Chair
of that Committee gave a briefing on the Committee ’s
work.
Great Lakes region
32. The Council convened an open meeting in April
with a briefing by the Special Envoy of the SecretaryGeneral for the Great Lakes Region, who described
efforts to implement the Peace, Security and
Cooperation Framework for the Democratic Republic of
the Congo and the Region.
33. In December, the Council adopted resolution 2389
(2017), in which it reiterated its call for States which
had signed the Framework to abide by the commitment
to non-interference in the internal affairs of
neighbouring countries and the prohibition against
abetting armed groups or war criminals. The Council
also encouraged enhanced cooperation among all
signatory States and urged enhanced regional
cooperation in countering armed groups.
Guinea-Bissau
34. In February, the Special Representative of the
Secretary-General for Guinea-Bissau briefed the
Council on the Secretary-General’s latest report and
18-14247

-----------------------Page 12 End-----------------------

Introduction

recent developments in Guinea-Bissau. The Chair of the
Guinea-Bissau configuration of the Peacebuilding
Commission also briefed the Council and statements
were made by the representatives of Guinea-Bissau and
Liberia on behalf of the Economic Community of West
African States (ECOWAS).
35. In February, the Council adopted resolution 2343
(2017), by which it renewed the mandate of the United
Nations Integrated Peacebuilding Office in GuineaBissau (UNIOGBIS) until 28 February 2018. The
Council fully endorsed the recommendations outlined in
the report of the Secretary-General regarding the need
for UNIOGBIS to refocus its efforts towards political
capacities in support of the Special Representative’s
good offices and political facilitation role.
36. In May, Council members received a briefing in
consultations from the Under-Secretary-General for
Political Affairs on Guinea-Bissau, focusing on the
ongoing political crisis. In a statement to the press
issued after the meeting, Council members called on
relevant stakeholders to strictly respect and comply with
the Conakry Agreement and the ECOWAS road map and
expressed their readiness to take necessary measures to
respond to a further worsening of the situation in
Guinea-Bissau.
37. In August, the Council received a briefing on the
Secretary-General’s report on Guinea-Bissau from the
Special Representative of the Secretary-General and
Head of UNIOGBIS. The Chair of the Committee
established pursuant to resolution 2048 (2012)
concerning Guinea-Bissau and the Chair of the GuineaBissau configuration of the Peacebuilding Commission
also gave briefings.
38. In September, the Council adopted a presidential
statement (S/PRST/2017/17) in which it called on the
leadership of Guinea-Bissau to implement the Conakry
Agreement of 14 October 2016.
Liberia
39. In June, the Council held a briefing followed by
consultations on the situation in Liberia. The Special
Representative of the Secretary-General and Head of the
United Nations Mission in Liberia (UNMIL) briefed the
Council on the latest report of the Secretary-General and
activities related to the Mission’s drawdown. The Chair
of the Liberia configuration of the Peacebuilding
Commission briefed the Council on his recent visit to
the country and developments relating to the
Peacebuilding Commission.

18-14247

40. In July, the Council adopted a presidential statement
(S/PRST/2017/11) on the Liberia peacebuilding plan
and the upcoming presidential elections.
41. In August, Council members held consultations on
the situation in Liberia. The Assistant Secretary-General
for Peacekeeping Operations briefed the Council on the
preparations for the presidential and legislative
elections to be held on 10 October in Liberia, as
requested in resolution 2333 (2016).
42. In December, the Special Representative for
Liberia and Head of UNMIL briefed Council members
in consultations. As mandated by resolution 2333
(2016), the Special Representative updated the Council
on the presidential elections in Liberia and the
drawdown of UNMIL towards its final withdrawal.
Libya
43. In February, the Council discussed the latest
situation in Libya, focusing on the efforts to reach an
inclusive and sustainable political settlement in Libya.
The Special Representative of the Secretary-General for
Libya and Head of the United Nations Support Mission
in Libya (UNSMIL) briefed the Council and said that,
despite some important advances in Libya, not enough
progress had been made in implementing the Libyan
Political Agreement forged in 2015. Terrorism remained
a threat, living conditions were poor and armed groups
continued to abuse human rights with impunity. During
a meeting in April, the Special Representative cautioned
that Libya risked a return to widespread conflict and
urged parties to adhere to the Libyan Political
Agreement.
44. In May, the Council issued a statement to the press,
in which it condemned the military escalation in
southern Libya including the recent attack at Birak
al-Shati’ and the escalation in violence in Tripoli. The
Council called on all parties to exercise restraint,
reiterated that there can be no military solution and
urged all parties to express their support for national
reconciliation.
45. In June, the Council adopted resolution 2357
(2017), extending the authorizations under which States,
obtaining the consent of the vessel’s flag State prior to
any inspections, may inspect vessels on the high seas off
the coast of Libya believed to be in violation of the arms
embargo, as set out in resolution 2292 (2016), for a
further 12 months, until 12 June 2018. At the end of
June, by resolution 2362 (2017), the Council extended
until 15 November 2018 the sanctions measures relating
to illicit petroleum exports, adding “attacks against
United Nations personnel” as criteria for the ongoing
travel ban and asset freeze measures.
11/234

-----------------------Page 13 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

46. In July, the Council issued a statement to the press,
in which it welcomed the appointment of the new
Special Representative of the Secretary-General,
Ghassan Salamé, and looked forward to supporting his
efforts to facilitate a political solution in Libya. The
Council also welcomed the meeting of Faiez
Serraj,President of the Presidency Council of Libya, and
General Khalifa Haftar, Commander of the Libyan
National Army, hosted in Paris by the President of
France on 25 July, and the resulting joint declaration in
which they urged all Libyans to support a negotiated
political solution, national reconciliation and an
immediate ceasefire.
47. In September, the Council extended for one year
the mandate of UNSMIL, as an integrated special
political mission, by resolution 2376 (2017).
48. In October, by resolution 2380 (2017), the Council
extended for a third one-year period the authorization,
established under resolution 2240 (2015), for Member
States to inspect, as permitted under international law,
on the high seas off the coast of Libya, vessels that have
been, are being, or imminently will be used by organized
criminal enterprises for migrant smuggling or human
trafficking from Libya.
49. In October, the Council adopted a presidential
statement on the situation in Libya (S/PRST/2017/19),
in which it endorsed the United Nations Action Plan for
Libya presented by the Special Representative of the
Secretary-General, in support of a Libyan-led transition
that would lead to the establishment of stable, unified,
representative and effective governance in the
framework of the Libyan Political Agreement, which
remained the only viable framework to end the Libyan
political crisis.
50. In November, the Special Representative briefed
the Council on the United Nations Action Plan for Libya
and UNSMIL efforts to amend the Libyan Political
Agreement, organize a national conference, prepare for
elections and provide humanitarian assistance. Given
the division among Libyans, the Special Representative
emphasized the need to rebuild the national polity,
without which effective institutions could not form.
51. In December, reports of migrants being sold into
slavery in Libya were also of grave concern to the
Council. In a presidential statement (S/PRST/2017/24)
issued in December, it condemned such actions as
heinous abuses of human rights and possible crimes
against humanity. Later that month, the Council
reiterated in a presidential statement (S/PRST/2017/26)
that the Libyan Political Agreement was the only viable
framework to end the political crisis and that its

12/234

implementation was essential for holding elections and
finalizing the transition.
52. Throughout the reporting period, the Chair of the
Committee established pursuant to resolution 1970
(2011) concerning Libya regularly briefed the Council
on the activities and decisions of the Committee.
53. The Council heard briefings from the Chief
Prosecutor of the International Criminal Court, in May
and November.
Mali
54. In January, the Council heard a briefing by the
Under-Secretary-General for Peacekeeping Operations,
who presented the latest report of the Secretary-General
on the situation in Mali, condemned the attack that
killed more than 50 people at a military base in Gao, and
called for speeding up implementation of the Agreement
on Peace and Reconciliation in Mali. The Minister for
Foreign Affairs, African Integration and International
Cooperation of Mali attended the meeting and addressed
the Council.
55. In April, the Council was briefed by the UnderSecretary-General for Peacekeeping Operations, who
presented the Secretary-General’s latest report and
provided an update on the situation in Mali, including
recent positive developments. He called for enhanced
efforts to combat transnational organized crime and to
implement the peace agreement, emphasized that
combating the accelerated activities of terrorists and the
transnational networks funding them continued to
require international support, and welcomed the
decision by the countries of the Group of 5 for the Sahel
(G-5 Sahel) to establish a regional force to combat such
activities. The Minister for Foreign Affairs of Mali
attended the meeting and addressed the Council.
56. In June, the Council held a briefing on Mali. The
Special Representative of the Secretary-General and
Head of the United Nations Multidimensional Integrated
Stabilization Mission in Mali (MINUSMA) reported
significant progress on implementation of the
Agreement on Peace and Reconciliation over the past
months, but agreed that terrorists and extremists were
gaining ground as existing tensions threatened to derail
achievements. The Minister for Foreign Affairs of Mali
emphasized that the Council’s authorization was
required to deploy a new joint counter-terrorism force
involving the G-5 Sahel States.
57. In October, the Special Representative of the
Secretary-General and Head of MINUSMA briefed the
Council. He said that all parties must redouble their
efforts to re-establish trust between them and, despite

18-14247

-----------------------Page 14 End-----------------------

Introduction

the prolonged delays in the implementation of the Peace
Agreement, engage in necessary reforms and provide a
peace dividend to the people. The Minister for Foreign
Affairs of Mali addressed the Council.
58. In 2017, the Council adopted two resolutions on
Mali, resolution 2364 (2017) in June, by which it
renewed the mandate of MINUSMA, and resolution
2374 (2017) in September, by which it established a
sanctions regime against individuals or entities that
threaten the peace, security or stability of Mali.
Somalia
59. In 2017, Somalia made significant progress in the
political process and security sector reform. At a
briefing in January the Council heard that the election
of a new Parliament in Somalia marked a milestone. In
May, the Deputy Special Representative of the
Secretary-General for Somalia added that peaceful
presidential elections in Somalia had created a unique
opportunity to forge a functional State.
60. With Somalia still facing the threat posed by
Al-Shabaab, drought and the humanitarian crisis, the
Council
issued
a
presidential
statement
(S/PRST/2017/3) in February, in which it called upon
the new President of Somalia to focus on the immediate
risk of famine and address the consequences of the
severe drought, and to accelerate political and security
reforms. In statements to the press issued in February
and June, the Council condemned terrorist attacks in
Mogadishu. In another press statement in August the
Council condemned a fatal attack by Al-Shabaab against
the Ugandan contingent of the African Union Mission in
Somalia (AMISOM), and in a press statement in
October it condemned a terrorist attack in Mogadishu in
which hundreds of civilians were killed. At a briefing in
September the Council was informed that hard-won
gains could be swept away without predictable funding
for AMISOM.
61. By resolution 2355 (2017), adopted in May, the
Council authorized States members of the African
Union to maintain the deployment of AMISOM until
31 August. On 30 August, by resolution 2372 (2017),
the Council extended its authorization of AMISOM until
31 May 2018, and approved a reduction of the level of
its uniformed personnel to a maximum of 21,626 by
31 December 2017, amid a transition of responsibilities
to Somali security forces. The Council emphasized that
the long-term objective for Somalia, with international
support, was for the Somali security forces to assume
full responsibility for security, recognizing that
AMISOM remained critical during the transition.

18-14247

62. In March, the Council adopted resolution 2346
(2017), by which it extended the mandate of the United
Nations Assistance Mission in Somalia (UNSOM) until
16 June. By resolution 2358 (2017), adopted in June, it
further extended the mandate of UNSOM until 31 March
2018.
63. In November, the Council adopted resolution 2385
(2017), by which it extended the modified arms embargo
on Somalia and the authorization for maritime
interdiction of illicit arms imports and charcoal exports
until 15 November 2018, and renewed the arms ban on
Eritrea for the same period. It also extended the mandate
of the Somalia and Eritrea Monitoring Group until
15 December 2018. The resolution was adopted by
11 votes in favour, with 4 abstentions.
64. Also in November, the Council adopted resolution
2383 (2017), by which it renewed for another year the
authorization for international naval forces to help to
fight piracy off the coast of Somalia.
Sudan and South Sudan
Darfur
65. The security situation in Darfur improved in 2017:
the armed groups have largely been defeated and there
was no new large-scale displacement. However, Darfur
still faced challenges in several areas, including the
political process and the humanitarian situation. During
the briefings held in January and April, the Council was
informed that, despite a decline in armed conflict,
civilians were still faced with violence and criminality,
while long-term solutions for 2.6 million displaced
persons remained elusive. It was suggested that the
mandate of the African Union-United Nations Hybrid
Operation in Darfur (UNAMID) should be amended. In
June, the Council was presented with the findings of a
strategic review recommending that the focus of
UNAMID should be adjusted.
66. In June the Council adopted resolution 2363
(2017), by which it renewed the mandate of UNAMID
until 30 June 2018, and endorsed the recommendations
in the strategic review, including a two-phase
restructuring of the mission that would lower troop and
police levels to 8,735 and 2,500, respectively. After that,
the Council held several meetings to follow up the
situation in Darfur and the reconfiguration of UNAMID.
67. The Council, in February, adopted resolution 2340
(2017), by which it extended until 12 March 2018 the
mandate of the Panel of Experts charged with
monitoring sanctions in Darfur. In 2017, the Council
was briefed several times by the Chair of the Committee

13/234

-----------------------Page 15 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

established pursuant
concerning the Sudan.

to

resolution

1591

(2005)

68. In June and December, the Council was briefed by
the Chief Prosecutor of the International Criminal
Court.
South Sudan
69. Throughout the year, the Council followed the
situation in South Sudan closely. There were more than
20 briefings or informal consultations. The Council
heard in briefings that progress towards ending
hostilities and resuming dialogue was limited, the
humanitarian situation was still dire, the national
dialogue
was
launched
in
May,
and
the
Intergovernmental Authority for Development (IGAD)
proposed to revitalize the Agreement on the Resolution
of the Conflict in the Republic of South Sudan, which
would hinge on the degree to which the Government and
opposition embraced it. The Council welcomed and
strongly supported the establishment by IGAD of the
High-level Revitalization Forum for the implementation
of the Agreement, and strongly urged all parties to
engage constructively in the process without
preconditions. As a first priority and a sign of
commitment, the Council called on all parties to end
hostilities as they had pledged.
70. In August, the Council sent letters to South
Sudanese parties (Government and opposition leaders)
in which the Council outlined its concerns, and in which
a message was conveyed to the parties to take steps to
cease offensives and engage with the efforts of IGAD
supported by the Council.
71. The Council issued two presidential statements
and several statements to the press. In the presidential
statement issued in March (S/PRST/2017/4), the
Council called for “immediate adherence to the
permanent ceasefire by all forces of the parties to the
conflict and all other armed groups”, stressed that there
was no military solution to the conflict, and condemned
reported human rights violations and attacks on
humanitarian workers. In the presidential statement
issued in December (S/PRST/2017/25) the Council
strongly supported the convening of an inclusive forum
as proposed by IGAD to get the Agreement back on
track and aim for substantive progress on the initiative
when it convened at the end of December. In press
statements in May and June, the Council condemned
attacks on peacekeepers of the United Nations Mission
in South Sudan (UNMISS) and UNAMID, including one
in which a Nigerian peacekeeper serving with UNAMID
was killed.

14/234

72. In May, the Council adopted resolution 2353
(2017), by which it renewed the sanctions measures
until 31 May 2018, including the travel ban and asset
freeze imposed by resolution 2206 (2015) on those
blocking peace, security and stability in South Sudan,
and also extended until 30 June 2018 the mandate of the
Panel of Experts overseeing sanctions.
73. In December, the Council, by resolution 2392
(2017), extended the mandate of UNMISS until
15 March 2018, and authorized the Mission to use all
necessary means to carry out its tasks as set out in
resolution 2327 (2016).
Sudan, South Sudan and Abyei
74. The Council had several meetings throughout the
year on the Abyei issue. During a briefing in October,
the Council was informed that the United Nations
Interim Security Force for Abyei (UNISFA) clearly
played a stabilizing role in Abyei.
75. In May, the Council adopted resolution 2352
(2017), by which it extended the mandate of UNISFA
until 15 November, including its tasks in support of the
disputed territory’s Joint Border Verification and
Monitoring Mechanism, while warning that the support
would be withdrawn unless the Sudan and South Sudan
complied fully with their obligations. It decided to
reduce the Force’s authorized troop ceiling from 5,326
to 4,791.
76. In November, recognizing some progress by the
parties, the Council duly responded by adopting
resolution 2386 (2017), by which it extended the Force’s
mandate until 15 May 2018, but limited support for the
Joint Border Verification and Monitoring Mechanism to
a further five months.
Western Sahara
77. In April, the Council adopted resolution 2351
(2017), by which it extended the mandate of the United
Nations Mission for the Referendum in Western Sahara
until 30 April 2018. Council members made statements
after the vote.

(UNOWAS) on the Secretary-General’s latest report on
the region. The meeting was followed by consultations
during which issues discussed included the political
crisis in the Gambia, the independent evaluation of the

United Nations Integrated Strategy for the Sahel and

79. The Council adopted a presidential statement
(S/PRST/2017/2) in January, in which it welcomed the

regarding the extension of the mandate of UNOWAS for

80. In July, the Special Representative and Head of
UNOWAS briefed the Council on recent developments
in the region and the semi-annual UNOWAS report.
Further discussions followed in consultations. Later in

(S/PRST/2017/10), in which it welcomed developments
in the Gambia and Côte d’Ivoire, highlighting the role
of UNOWAS in supporting, as necessary, Côte d’Ivoire
following the recent withdrawal of the United Nations

of UNOWAS collaboration.
Peace consolidation in West Africa: the Gambia
81. The Council followed closely the situation in the
Gambia. In January, the Council adopted resolution
2337 (2017) on the presidential elections in the Gambia,
in which it endorsed the decisions of ECOWAS and the
African Union to recognize Adama Barrow as the
legitimate President of the country.
82. The Council in January also adopted a presidential

the independent evaluation of the United Nations
Integrated Strategy for the Sahel. Following the
adoption of the statement, the Under-Secretary-General
for Political Affairs updated members on the quickly
evolving situation in the Gambia, under “any other
business”, during consultations.
83. Later in January, the Council received a follow-up
briefing on the Gambia from the Special Representative
and Head of UNOWAS, which included a discussion of
the joint declaration issued by ECOWAS, the African
Union and the United Nations following the agreement
of the former President, Yahya Jammeh, to transfer
power to Mr. Barrow and to leave the country.

Peace and security in Africa: Lake Chad basin
84. In January, the Council received briefings from the
Assistant Secretary-General for Political Affairs and the
Under-Secretary-General for Humanitarian Affairs and
Emergency Relief Coordinator on the Lake Chad basin
crisis precipitated by the Boko Haram conflict. The
Council also heard a briefing from a civil society
representative. A number of Council members expressed
support for a proposed Council mission to the Lake
Chad basin region.
85. In March, the Council adopted its first resolution
(2349 (2017)) addressing the situation in the Lake Chad
basin region, including the security, humanitarian and
peacebuilding aspects of the crisis.
Peace and security in Africa: Sahel
86. In June, the Council adopted resolution 2359
(2017), in which it welcomed the deployment of the
Joint Force of the Group of Five for the Sahel
throughout the territories of its contributing countries,
with up to 5,000 military and police personnel, with a
view to restoring peace and security in the Sahel region.
87. In August, the Council was briefed by the
Assistant-Secretary-General
for
Peacekeeping
Operations on the activities of the G-5 Sahel Joint
Force; he emphasized that the Joint Force to combat
cross-border terrorism and organized crime could make
a significant contribution to stabilizing the region by
working in tandem with other ongoing initiatives.
88. In October, the Council heard briefings on the
activities of the G-5 Sahel Joint Force by the SecretaryGeneral, the Minister for Foreign Affairs, African
Integration and International Cooperation of Mali, the
Chairperson of the African Union Commission, and the
European Union Special Representative for the Sahel.
Ministers from Burkina Faso, Chad, Mauritania and the
Niger also attended the meeting and addressed the
Council.
89. In December, the Council adopted resolution 2391
(2017), in which it requested the Secretary-General to
conclude a technical agreement among the United
Nations, the European Union and the G-5 Sahel States,
with a view to providing operational and logistical
support through MINUSMA to the joint force
conducting cross-border counter-terrorist operations
across the region.
Peace and security in Africa: briefing on the
African Union
90. In February, the Council held consultations and
was briefed by the Secretary-General. The main topic
15/234

-----------------------Page 17 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

was the outcome of the Secretary-General’s attendance
at the African Union summit and his interactions with

Peace and security in Africa: visit of the Deputy

91. In August, the Deputy Secretary-General briefed

mission to Nigeria and the Democratic Republic of the

Peace and security in Africa: enhancing African
capacities in the areas of peace and security
92. In July, the Council held an open debate on “Peace
and security in Africa: enhancing African capacities in
the areas of peace and security”. The Council was
briefed by the Secretary-General and the African Union
Commissioner for Peace and Security. The main topic
was how to enhance African peace and security
capacities and the partnership between the United
Nations and the African Union in the areas of peace and
security. In the ensuing debate, consensus was reached
by the speakers that deeper cooperation between the
United Nations and the African Union needed to be
enhanced to help to promote the peacekeeping
operations of the African Union.

Haiti
98. The Council held a debate on the situation in Haiti

contribute to the country’s stability and development.

Asia
Afghanistan
102. The situation in Afghanistan remained fragile in
2017. It was stressed that Afghanistan needed continued
international support in order to face its multitude of
challenges.
103. The security situation remained dangerous, and
civilians continued to bear the brunt of the violence. The
presence of Islamic State in Iraq and the Levant (ISIL,
also known as Da’esh) and foreign terrorist fighters
complicated the situation, and a series of terrorist
attacks was carried out in Afghanistan.
104. Progress had been made in organizing
parliamentary and district council elections scheduled
for 2018, and many Council members urged regional
support for tackling all of the challenges faced by
Afghanistan.
105. On 17 March, the Council adopted resolution 2344
(2017), by which it extended the mandate of the United
Nations Assistance Mission in Afghanistan until
17 March 2018.
106. The Council held quarterly debates on Afghanistan
in March, June, September and December. In the open
debate in December, the Executive Director of the
United Nations Office on Drugs and Crime voiced alarm
over the unprecedented high levels of opium poppy
cultivation and production in Afghanistan.
Democratic People’s Republic of Korea
107. In December, the Council held a meeting on the
situation in the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea.
The agenda for the meeting was adopted after a
procedural vote of 10 in favour to 3 against, with 2
abstentions. The Assistant Secretary-General for
Political Affairs and the United Nations High
Commissioner for Human Rights briefed the Council on
the humanitarian and human rights situation in the
Democratic People’s Republic of Korea during 2017,
noting the lack of progress, and expressed their concerns
and observations.

18-14247

Myanmar
108. The situation in Rakhine State, Myanmar,
deteriorated rapidly after the attacks against Myanmar
security forces carried out by the Arakan Rohingya
Salvation Army on 25 August in Rakhine State.
109. At a briefing in September, the Secretary-General
informed the Council that at least 500,000 civilians had
fled their homes and sought refuge in Bangladesh. He
urged the Government of Myanmar to take three
immediate steps: end military operations, allow
unfettered access for humanitarian support and ensure
the voluntary, safe and dignified return of displaced
Rohingya to their homes.
110. The Council issued a presidential statement
(S/PRST/2017/22) in November, in which it condemned
the violence and reports of human rights violations and
abuses in Rakhine State, expressed alarm at the
deteriorating humanitarian situation and called on the
Government of Myanmar to ensure no further excessive
use of violence and restore civilian administration and
intercommunal harmony in Rakhine State. The Council
also urged the implementation of agreed mechanisms to
allow the voluntary, safe and dignified return of those
who had fled and to ensure access for humanitarian aid.
111. As previously requested by the Council, the
Secretary-General briefed the Council in December on
developments in the situation in Rakhine State.

recent six-month report on the implementation of the
peace agreement, expressing concern about the lack of
progress in addressing the need for real reforms.
113. In November, the Council held its debate on
Bosnia and Herzegovina and adopted resolution 2384
(2017), by which it renewed the authorization of the
multinational stabilization force (EUFOR-Althea) for an
additional year. The High Representative briefed the
Council on the latest developments.

Middle East
The situation in the Middle East, including the
Palestinian question
121. The Council held monthly briefings under the
agenda item on the situation in the Middle East,
including the Palestinian question. The Special
Coordinator for the Middle East Peace Process, highlevel officials from the Department of Political Affairs
of the Secretariat and other invited guests briefed the
Council with a general concern for the aggregate
situation on the ground.
122. Following the adoption of resolution 2334 (2016),
the international community did not see the shift in
behaviour among the parties that was called for in the
resolution. Instead, the international community
continued to witness the expansion of settlement
activities, the demolition of Palestinian private
properties and increasing violence between Israel and
Palestine. These assessments were orally reported by the
Special Coordinator to the Council in March, June,
September and December.
123. In mid-July, an emergency consultation was
convened by the Secretariat, in response to the
immediate and dire humanitarian situation in the Gaza
Strip. The Special Coordinator briefed the Council on
the alarming humanitarian situation in the Gaza Strip.
Members of the Council called for urgent action by all
relevant parties in order to alleviate the suffering of the
people in Gaza and welcomed the continued external
support provided to Gaza. In November, speaking in
reference to an intra-Palestinian reconciliation
agreement reached in October, the Special Coordinator
said that a genuine change in Gaza, including full
Palestinian Authority control over security, would help
to restore confidence in the feasibility of a
comprehensive peace agreement.
124. The Jerusalem situation drew the attention of the
Council throughout the year. In July, the Council
discussed the situation in Jerusalem in consultations.
The Special Coordinator briefed the Council on the
latest developments with regard to the Haram al-Sharif.
Council members expressed their grave concerns about
the extremely fragile situation, the risk of escalation and
the unfolding violence in the Old City of Jerusalem.
Political and diplomatic efforts and utmost restraint
were called for in order to calm the situation and avoid
further escalation.
125. Late in July, the Council held an open debate,
during which the Special Coordinator warned the
Council that developments at holy sites in Jerusalem
over the previous days had demonstrated the grave risk
18-14247

-----------------------Page 20 End-----------------------

Introduction

of a dangerous escalation, including the transformation
of the conflict into a religious one, emphasizing the risk
of dragging Israel and Palestine and the rest of the
region into a vortex of violence. He called for a focus
on the need to restore a political perspective, to bring
Israelis and Palestinians back into an environment
conducive to negotiations on a final status arrangement
and to avoid turning the national Israeli-Palestinian
conflict into a religious one. Council members and
speakers expressed their views.
126. A briefing was held in December, following the
announcement by the United States of America of its
decision to recognize Jerusalem as the capital of Israel
and to move its embassy to that city from Tel Aviv. The
Special Coordinator reported that the position of the
United Nations on the status of Jerusalem had not
changed: it remained a final status issue for which a
comprehensive, just and lasting solution must be
achieved through negotiations between the parties, and
on the basis of relevant United Nations resolutions and
other agreements. Expressing concern about the potential
risk of a violent escalation, he called on all parties to
refrain from engaging in provocative actions and rhetoric.
Council members exchanged views on the issue.
127. Later in December in a briefing and consultations,
the Special Coordinator noted with regret that there had
not been significant positive moves towards advancing
peace, and the parties remained more divided than ever.
Following the consultations, the Council took action on
a draft resolution tabled by Egypt on the status of
Jerusalem. The Council failed to adopt the draft
resolution owing to the negative vote of one permanent
member of the Council, leading to a recorded result of
14 in favour and 1 against.
The situation in the Middle East
(Syrian Arab Republic)
128. The Syrian Arab Republic was a significant focus
for the Council in 2017. Meetings were held on the
chemical weapons track, the humanitarian track and the
political track nearly every month.
129. In February, the Under-Secretary-General and
High Representative for Disarmament Affairs and the
Head of the Organisation for the Prohibition of
Chemical Weapons-United Nations Joint Investigative
Mechanism briefed Council members on chemical
weapons in the Syrian Arab Republic. The Council
failed to adopt a draft resolution seeking to impose
sanctions on individuals and entities associated with the
Government of the Syrian Arab Republic. In April, the
Council held a briefing on the reports of alleged use of
chemical weapons in the Syrian Arab Republic,

18-14247

following the chemical weapons attack in the Khan
Shaykhun area of Idlib. The Council failed to adopt a
draft resolution relating to the Khan Shaykhun attack.
From August, the renewal of the mandate of the Joint
Investigative Mechanism received particular attention
by the Council. In October and November, the Council
failed to adopt four draft resolutions by which it would
have renewed the mandate of the Mechanism before its
expiration on 17 November.
130. Throughout the year, a series of meetings was held
in Geneva and Astana as part of the intra-Syrian talks
and Astana dialogues. Nearly every month, the Special
Envoy of the Secretary-General briefed the Council on
the progress of the intra-Syrian talks. In January, the
Council issued a statement to the press on the
international meeting on the Syrian Arab Republic in
Astana. The Council issued another statement to the
press in March, mainly welcoming the announcement by
the Special Envoy of a clear agenda for future
negotiations, in accordance with resolution 2254 (2015).
At the fourth round of Astana talks in Kazakhstan, the
Russian Federation, the Islamic Republic of Iran, and
Turkey signed an agreement to create four
“de-escalation zones” in the Syrian Arab Republic,
which came into effect on 6 May.
131. The Council continued to receive monthly
briefings on the implementation of resolutions 2139
(2014), 2165 (2014) and 2191 (2014) from the UnderSecretary-General for Humanitarian Affairs and other
officials from the Office for the Coordination of
Humanitarian Affairs, who introduced the reports of the
Secretary-General and detailed the humanitarian
situation in the Syrian Arab Republic and the cross-line
and cross-border humanitarian assistance. In December,
the Council adopted resolution 2393 (2017), which
renewed for a further period of one year the
authorization established by the Council in resolution
2165 (2014) regarding cross-border and cross-line
humanitarian access to the Syrian Arab Republic.
132. In April, the Council released a statement to the
press condemning the terrorist attack in Rashidin,
Syrian Arab Republic.
Iraq
133. In almost every month in 2017, the Council
discussed various issues on Iraq, including the fight
against ISIL, national reconciliation, chemical weapons
issues, the humanitarian situation in Mosul and the plans
of the Kurdistan Regional Government to unilaterally
hold a referendum.
134. In February and July, the Council issued
statements to the press, in which it condemned terrorist
19/234

-----------------------Page 21 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

attacks in Iraq. In September, the Council issued a
statement to the press expressing concern over the
potentially destabilizing impact of the plans of the
Kurdistan Regional Government to unilaterally hold a
referendum and, in October, it issued another expressing

Lebanon
137. The Council met regularly on Lebanon, including
in consultations on resolutions 1559 (2004) and 1701
(2006) and on the operations of the United Nations
Interim Force in Lebanon (UNIFIL). In August, by
resolution 2373 (2017), the Council extended the
mandate of UNIFIL until 31 August 2018. The Council
further authorized UNIFIL to take the steps necessary to
ensure that its area of operations was not used for hostile
activities of any kind, and requested the SecretaryGeneral to explore ways to enhance the Force’s efforts
by increasing its visible presence.
138. The Council issued a statement to the press in
December in which it welcomed the return of the Prime
Minister, Saad Hariri, to Lebanon and his decision to
continue his term. The Council also welcomed previous
efforts, which contributed to the resumption of the
Council of Ministers of Lebanon on 5 December. The
Council also reaffirmed its support for the ongoing
efforts of the Lebanese authorities to restore normal
functioning of institutions and prepare for the holding
of legislative elections by May 2018, and encouraged
the international community to continue and increase its
support for the political, social and economic stability
of Lebanon.
United Nations Disengagement Observer Force
139. The Council met regularly on the United Nations
Disengagement Observer Force (UNDOF). It adopted
resolution 2361 (2017) in June, renewing the mandate
of UNDOF for an additional six months, and resolution
2394 (2017) in December, by which it renewed the
mandate for a further six months, until 30 June 2018. In
those resolutions, the Council stressed the importance of
full adherence to the Agreement on Disengagement of
20/234

Forces of 1974 and called upon both sides to exercise
maximum restraint. The Council welcomed the progress
made in returning UNDOF to its former headquarters at
Camp Faouar, on the Bravo side of the area of
separation.
Yemen
140. Throughout 2017, the continuing and escalated
armed conflict in Yemen brought a deteriorating security
situation and increased fragmentation and division
among parties. As a result, the humanitarian crisis
continuously worsened, with an outbreak of cholera and
increasing threats of famine, and the expansion of
Al-Qaida in the Arabian Peninsula and ISIL in Yemen
was also witnessed. Despite the commitment and
tireless efforts of the Special Envoy of the SecretaryGeneral for Yemen to bring the parties to negotiations
with a view to ending the conflict in Yemen, there was
unfortunately no significant progress on resuming
political talks.
141. In February, by adopting resolution 2342 (2017),
the Council renewed for one year, until 26 February
2018, the targeted sanctions measures imposed by
resolution 2140 (2014).
142. In
June,
in
a
presidential
statement
(S/PRST/2017/7), the Council expressed grave concern
about the devastating humanitarian impact of the
conflict on civilians and called on all parties to engage
constructively with the Special Envoy’s proposals for
increasing commercial and humanitarian shipments
through Red Sea ports, in order to allow safe, rapid and
unhindered access for humanitarian supplies and
personnel to all affected governorates. The Council also
called on the parties to immediately agree on the
modalities for a durable cessation of hostilities and to
engage in peace talks in a flexible and constructive
manner, without preconditions and in good faith.
143. In December, the Council issued a statement to the
press, in which it condemned the ballistic missile attack
by Houthi forces against Saudi Arabia, stressing that all
Member States must fully implement all aspects of the
arms embargo, as required.

Thematic issues
Briefing by the President of the International Court
of Justice
144. In October, the Council was briefed by the
President of the International Court of Justice. The
President outlined the overall work of the Court,
focusing on the contributions of the Court to the

18-14247

-----------------------Page 22 End-----------------------

Introduction

strategic priorities of working for peace, supporting
sustainable development and reforming the internal
management of the United Nations.
Briefing by the United Nations High Commissioner
for Refugees
145. In November, the Council was briefed by the
United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, who
said that the number of forcibly displaced people around
the world was now approaching 66 million, and stressed
that a full response to the prevailing mass displacement
could be achieved only through action to restore
security, resolve conflict and build peace.
Children and armed conflict
146. During the open debate on children and armed
conflict held in October, the Council reiterated its strong
condemnation of all violations of applicable
international law involving the recruitment and use of
children by parties to armed conflict, killing and
maiming, rape and other forms of sexual violence,
abductions, attacks against schools and hospitals and
denial of humanitarian access by parties to armed
conflict.
Issuing
a
presidential
statement
(S/PRST/2017/21), the Council stated that the
protection of children in armed conflict should be an
important aspect of any comprehensive strategy to
resolve conflict and sustain peace, and remained deeply
concerned over the lack of progress on the ground where
parties to conflict continued to violate with impunity the
relevant provisions of applicable international law
relating to the rights and protection of children. It also
called upon all parties to armed conflict to allow and
facilitate safe, timely and unhindered humanitarian
access to children.
Cooperation between the United Nations and
regional and subregional organizations in
maintaining international peace and security
147. The Council convened several open briefings and
was briefed by the Chair-in-Office of OSCE in
February; the High Representative of the European
Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy in May;
the Chef de Cabinet of the Executive Office of the
Secretary-General, the Commissioner for Peace and
Security of the African Union and the African Union
High Representative for the Peace Fund in June; and the
Special Representative of the Secretary-General to the
African Union and Head of the United Nations Office to
the African Union in September.

18-14247

Election of five members of the International Court
of Justice
148. In November, following several simultaneous
rounds of voting in the Council and in the General
Assembly, the President of the Council announced that
both the Council and the General Assembly had agreed
on the following four candidates: Mr. Ronny Abraham,
Mr. Antônio Augusto Cançado Trindade, Mr. Nawaf
Salam and Mr. Abdulqawi Ahmed Yusuf. Thus they
were elected as members of the International Court of
Justice for a term of office of nine years, beginning on
6 February 2018.
149. After several further rounds of voting, the
President of the Council was notified by the United
Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland of its
decision to withdraw the nomination of Mr. Christopher
Greenwood. The Council and the General Assembly
then simultaneously held one further round of voting,
also in November, and elected Mr. Dalveer Bhandari to
the International Court of Justice for a term of office of
nine years, beginning on 6 February 2018.
General issues relating to sanctions
150. In August, the Council held a briefing on general
issues relating to sanctions. The Assistant SecretaryGeneral for Political Affairs addressed the meeting, and
Council members expressed views on the subject.
International Tribunals for Rwanda and the
Former Yugoslavia
151. In June, the Council heard a briefing by the
President of the International Tribunal for the
Prosecution of Persons Responsible for Serious
Violations of International Humanitarian Law
Committed in the Territory of the Former Yugoslavia
since 1991, the President of the International Residual
Mechanism for Criminal Tribunals and the Prosecutor
of the Tribunal and the Mechanism, during which
concerns about war crimes denial and failure to
cooperate with national authorities were expressed.
Some Council members called for bolstered efforts to
apprehend the eight fugitives indicted by the
International Criminal Tribunal for Rwanda.
152. In December, the Council was briefed by the
President of the International Tribunal for the Former
Yugoslavia, the President of the Mechanism and the
Prosecutor of the Tribunal and the Mechanism on their
work. Some Council members commended the
accomplishments of the Tribunal; one member raised
the issue of double standards in the Tribunal.

21/234

-----------------------Page 23 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

153. The Tribunal completed its work on 31 December
and the Council issued a press statement to mark that
closure.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
conflict prevention and sustaining peace
154. The Council convened an open debate on conflict
prevention and sustaining peace in January, during
which the Secretary-General gave his first address to the
Council since taking office, setting restoring trust and
preventing crises as United Nations priorities.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
conflicts in Europe
155. In February, the Council held an open debate on
the maintenance of international peace and security.
With the Minister for Foreign Affairs of Ukraine
presiding, the meeting sought to assess existing threats
to regional and international peace and security caused
by conflicts in Europe. It was also intended to review
responses to ongoing instability and to dangerous
developments within European countries in postconflict or protracted conflict situations. The SecretaryGeneral, the Secretary-General of OSCE and the
Secretary-General of the European External Action
Service of the European Union briefed the Council.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
destruction and trafficking of cultural heritage by
terrorist groups and in situations of armed conflict
156. In March, the Council held a briefing on the
unlawful destruction of cultural heritage, religious sites
and artefacts and the smuggling of cultural property by
terrorist groups during armed conflict. The Council
adopted resolution 2347 (2017) before the briefing, in
which it recalled its condemnation of any engagement
in trade involving ISIL, the Nusrah Front and all other
individuals or groups associated with Al-Qaida.
157. In November, the Council held a briefing on
destruction of world heritage sites and illicit trafficking
in cultural objects to finance terrorism and criminal
networks by terrorists, particularly in armed conflict
situations.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
general issues
158. The Council convened two meetings in March and
October, highlighting the alarming levels of food
insecurity and threat of famine in north-eastern Nigeria,
Somalia, South Sudan and Yemen and issued a
presidential statement (S/PRST/2017/14) in August, in
which it expressed its grave concern about the
22/234

unprecedented level of global humanitarian needs and
the threat of famine currently facing more than
20 million people in those countries or areas. The
Council further emphasized that ongoing conflicts and
violence have devastating humanitarian consequences
and hinder an effective humanitarian response in the
short, medium and long term and are therefore a major
cause of famine in the above-mentioned situations, and
called upon all parties to urgently take steps that would
enable a more effective humanitarian response.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
human rights and prevention of armed conflict
159. In April, the Council heard a briefing on
maintenance of international peace and security by the
Secretary-General, with a focus on human rights and
prevention of armed conflict.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
mine action
160. In June, there was a briefing by the Assistant
Secretary-General for Rule of Law and Security
Institutions and an official of the Mine Action Service
in Colombia on a comprehensive approach to mine
action and explosive hazard threat mitigation. Council
members called upon all parties to work together to
address the humanitarian concerns arising from
landmines, explosive remnants of war and improvised
explosive devices.
161. In June, the Council adopted resolution 2365
(2017), in which it expressed grave concern over the
threat that landmines, explosive remnants of war and
improvised explosive devices posed to civilians and
stressed the need to take appropriate measures to
mitigate that danger effectively.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
preventive diplomacy and transboundary waters
162. The Council convened an open debate in June
highlighting the scarcity of water and the need for
cooperation in managing transboundary water
resources.
Maintenance of international peace and security:
trafficking in persons in situations of conflict
163. The Council held two open debates on trafficking
in persons in situations of conflict, in March and
November, adopted resolution 2388 (2017) in
November and convened two briefings on the matter in
November.

18-14247

-----------------------Page 24 End-----------------------

Introduction

Maintenance of international peace and security:
addressing complex contemporary challenges to
international peace and security
164. The Council held an open debate in December to
discuss how it could better address complex
contemporary challenges to international peace and
security. The Secretary-General briefed the Council.
Non-proliferation of weapons of mass destruction
165. In March, the Council was briefed by the Chair of
the Committee established pursuant to resolution 1540
(2004) on the work of the Committee. Council members
expressed concern about the likelihood that non-State
actors, particularly terrorist groups, could acquire,
manufacture or use weapons of mass destruction.
166. In June, the Council held an open debate on the
global effort to prevent the proliferation of weapons of
mass destruction by non-State actors. The Council heard
statements highlighting proliferation threats, including
relevant developments in science and technology,
intangible technology transfer and proliferation
financing.
167. In September, the Council held a ministerial-level
meeting on the proliferation of weapons of mass
destruction. Council members discussed ways to
strengthen efforts to prevent proliferation of weapons of
mass destruction.
Non-proliferation: Islamic Republic of Iran
168. In January, June and December, the Council held
three open briefings. The Under-Secretary-General for
Political Affairs gave a briefing on the reports of the
Secretary-General on the implementation of resolution
2231 (2015). The Security Council Facilitator for the
implementation of resolution 2231 (2015) gave a
briefing on his reports on the implementation of
resolution 2231 (2015). A representative of the
European Union delegation to the United Nations gave
a briefing on the implementation of the Joint
Comprehensive Plan of Action.
169. In January and April, two informal consultations
were held by the Council members on the ballistic
missile test and space launch conducted by the Islamic
Republic of Iran.
Non-proliferation: Democratic People’s Republic
of Korea
170. In April and December, the Council held two
ministerial-level
meetings
on
the
theme
“Non-proliferation: Democratic People’s Republic of
Korea”. In September, the Council had one open
18-14247

briefing on the nuclear test conducted by the Democratic
People’s Republic of Korea. In July and November, the
Council had two open briefings on the missile launches
conducted by the Democratic People’s Republic of
Korea.
171. Urgent informal consultations were held in
February, March, May, August and September,
following the continuation of ballistic missile launches
conducted by the Democratic People’s Republic of
Korea, in violation of relevant Council resolutions.
172. The Council adopted resolution 2356 (2017) in
June in response to a series of ballistic missile launches
and other activities conducted by the Democratic
People’s Republic of Korea since 9 September 2016.
173. The Council adopted resolution 2371 (2017) in
August in response to the ballistic missile launches by
the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea on 3 July
and 28 July, which the Democratic People’s Republic of
Korea had stated were launches of intercontinental
ballistic missiles.
174. The Council adopted resolution 2375 (2017) in
September in response to the nuclear test conducted by
the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea on
2 September.
175. The Council adopted resolution 2397 (2017) in
December in response to the ballistic missile launch
conducted by the Democratic People’s Republic of
Korea on 28 November.
176. On 29 August, the Council issued a presidential
statement (S/PRST/2017/16) in response to the ballistic
missile launch conducted by the Democratic People’s
Republic of Korea on 28 August, as well as the multiple
ballistic missile launches conducted by the Democratic
People’s Republic of Korea on 25 August.
177. The Council issued eight press statements
throughout the year on the ballistic missile launches and
engine test conducted by the Democratic People’s
Republic of Korea.
178. The Chair of the Committee established pursuant
to resolution 1718 (2006) gave briefings to Council
members on the work of the Committee in February,
May and September. The Council adopted resolution
2345 (2017) in March, by which it extended the mandate
of the Panel of Experts and expanded it to include the
new measures adopted in resolution 2321 (2016).
Peacebuilding and sustaining peace
179. In June, the Council held the annual briefing on
peacebuilding and sustaining peace. The Chair of the

23/234

-----------------------Page 25 End-----------------------

Report of the Security Council to the General Assembly for 2017

Peacebuilding Commission briefed the Council on the
work of the Peacebuilding Commission.
Protection of civilians in armed conflict
180. The Council convened an open debate in May,
with a focus on protection of civilians and medical care
in armed conflict, following the submission of the report
of the Secretary-General on the protection of civilians
in armed conflict.
Small arms
181. In December, the Under-Secretary-General and
High Representative for Disarmament Affairs gave a
briefing on small arms. The Council members stressed
the importance of global action to prevent and combat
the illicit trade in small arms and light weapons and
supported the full implementation of the Programme of
Action to Prevent, Combat and Eradicate the Illicit
Trade in Small Arms and Light Weapons in All Its
Aspects and the International Tracing Instrument.
Threats to international peace and security caused
by terrorist acts
182. In February, the Council was briefed by the UnderSecretary-General for Political Affairs on United
Nations counter-terrorism efforts in support of Member
States against ISIL, followed by consultations. The
briefing was based on the strategic-level report of the
Secretary-General, submitted pursuant to resolution
2253 (2015).
183. In February, the Council held an open debate on
the protection of critical infrastructure against terrorist
attacks and called upon Member States to address the
risk of terrorist attacks against critical infrastructure.
The Council adopted resolution 2341 (2017) before the
open debate.
184. In May, the Council adopted resolution 2354
(2017), in which it welcomed the new framework
developed by its Counter-Terrorism Committee and
urged Member States to follow a number of concrete
guidelines aimed at countering the narratives used by
terrorist groups and amplifying positive and credible
alternatives to audiences vulnerable to extremist
messages.
185. In July, the Council adopted resolution 2368
(2017), by which it renewed and updated the ISIL and
Al-Qaida sanctions regime, pursuant to resolutions 1267
(1999), 1989 (2011) and 2253 (2015). The resolution is
an update to resolution 2253 (2015), in which the
Council decided that it would review the sanctions

24/234

measures “with a view to their possible further
strengthening” within 18 months of adoption.
186. In August, the Council held a briefing on the theme
“Threats to international peace and security: preventing
terrorists from acquiring weapons”. The Council
adopted resolution 2370 (2017), in which it reaffirmed
its previous decision in resolution 1373 (2001) that all
States should refrain from providing any form of
support to those involved in terrorist acts, including by
eliminating the supply of weapons to terrorists.
187. In August, the Council issued a presidential
statement (S/PRST/2017/15), in which it affirmed that,
having reviewed the implementation of the measures
outlined in resolution 2255 (2015) on its sanctions
against Taliban affiliates, no further adjustments were
necessary at that time.
188. In September, the Council adopted resolution 2379
(2017), in which it requested the Secretary-General to
establish an independent investigative team headed by a
Special Adviser, to support domestic efforts to hold ISIL
accountable for its actions in Iraq.
189. In September, the Chair of the Counter-Terrorism
Committee and the Secretary-General of the
International Civil Aviation Organization, briefed the
Council on security in civil aviation at an open meeting.
190. In September, the Council held a briefing on
strengthening the coordination and coherence of
counter-terrorism efforts through the Office of CounterTerrorism. The Under-Secretary-General for CounterTerrorism, the Chair of the Counter-Terrorism
Committee and the Officer-in-Charge of the CounterTerrorism Committee Executive Directorate briefed the
Council on related issues.
191. In November, the Council held a briefing on
returning foreign terrorist fighters and called upon the
international community to step up cooperation to
address the complex problem of foreign terrorist
fighters returning home or travelling to other regions.
192. In December, the Council adopted resolution 2395
(2017) to renew the mandate of the Counter-Terrorism
Committee Executive Directorate, the expert body that
supports the Council’s counter-terrorism architecture,
until 31 December 2021.
193. In December, the Council adopted resolution 2396
(2017), expressed concern about the issue of foreign
terrorist fighters connected to ISIL, the Nusrah Front
and other cells, affiliates, splinter groups or derivatives,
and urged Member States to step up implementation of
resolution 2178 (2014).

18-14247

-----------------------Page 26 End-----------------------

Introduction

United Nations peacekeeping operations
194. In 2017, the Council held five briefings and two
open debates on United Nations peacekeeping operations
and adopted two resolutions, resolutions 2378 (2017)
and 2382 (2017). The Council also adopted a presidential
statement
(S/PRST/2017/27)
on
peacekeeping
operations, as well as a press statement on environmental
management of peacekeeping operations.
195. In April, the Council was briefed by the SecretaryGeneral on the United Nations peacekeeping operatio ns
review. In October and December, the Council was
briefed on strategic force generation and on capacity
gaps in United Nations peacekeeping operations by the
Under-Secretary-General and the Assistant SecretaryGeneral for Peacekeeping Operations, respectively. In
May and November, the Council held its annual
dialogues with United Nations peacekeeping operations
Force Commanders and Police Commissioners,
respectively.
196. In August, the Council held an open debate to
discuss the potential contribution of United Nations
peacekeeping operations to the overarching goal of
sustaining peace. The Deputy Secretary-General, the
former Chair of the Advisory Group of Experts on the
Review of the Peacebuilding Architecture and a member
of the High-level Independent Panel on Peace
Operations briefed the Council.
197. In September, the Council held a high-level open
debate on United Nations peacekeeping operations
reform and was briefed by the Secretary-General, the
Chairperson of the African Union Commission and the
former Chair of the High-level Independent Panel on
Peace Operations.
Women and peace and security
198. In May, the Council held an open debate on sexual
violence in conflicts. The Deputy Secretary-General and
the Under-Secretary-General and Special Adviser to the
Secretary-General on the Prevention of Genocide
briefed the Council. The objective of the debate was to
raise awareness of the issue of sexual violence in
conflict as a tactic of war and terrorism, focusing
particularly on patterns and trends identified in the
annual report of the Secretary-General on conflictrelated sexual violence.
199. In October, the Council held its annual open
debate on women and peace and security. The Chef de
Cabinet of the Secretary-General, the Executive
Director of UN-Women, the Secretary-General of the
International Organization of la Francophonie and a
civil society representative briefed the Council. Member

18-14247

States welcomed the progress made since the adoption
of resolution 1325 (2000) regarding the normative
frameworks to empower and protect women, called for
further implementation of the women and peace and
security agenda and emphasized the importance of
women’s participation.

Other matters
Annual report of the Security Council
200. On 9 August, the Council adopted its annual report
for the period from 1 August 2015 to 31 December 2016.
The delegation of Japan, as the President of the Council
for the month of July 2016, was responsible for
coordinating the preparation of the introduction to the
draft report and noted that it covered the 17-month
period as a transitional arrangement and that, as
stipulated in the note by the President dated
10 December 2015 (S/2015/944), the period of coverage
for all future reports would be from 1 January to
31 December. On 28 August, the Permanent
Representative of Egypt, in his capacity as President of
the Council for the month, presented the annual report
to the General Assembly.
Implementation of the note by the President of the
Security Council (S/2010/507)
201. The Council held two formal wrap-up sessions in
February and August, to review its work and consider
how it could improve its efficiency and effectiveness, in
particular in dealing with country-specific situations,
conflict prevention and its working methods.
202. By the end of August, the Council had reached
agreement on a new version of the compendium of its
working methods, commonly referred to as “note 507”.
The document was prepared under the leadership of
Japan, in its capacity as Chair of the Informal Working
Group on Documentation and Other Procedural
Questions, in which most discussions on the Council’s
working methods are held. On 30 August, the Council
issued a press statement on the adoption of the revised
note (S/2017/507).
Subsidiary bodies
203. In May, the three Chairs of the terrorism-related
committees briefed the Council on their ongoing duties,
cooperation and goals for the future.
204. In December, the six outgoing non-permanent
members (Egypt, Italy, Japan, Senegal, Ukraine and
Uruguay) briefed the Council on the work of the
committees that they had chaired.
